Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
The General Rule: Intention in Worship and Transactions
Explaining when intention affects validity and reward of actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' to distinguish when intention is required for validity versus only for reward across the categories of mubah, sunnah, wajib, and mu'amalat.

Core Idea
Intention determines reward in all actions, but only affects validity in acts of worship intended for their own sake.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym demonstrates how the great maxim governs both worship and worldly transactions. By understanding the classification of actions and when intention matters for validity versus reward, you gain a critical tool for fatwa. The principle is straightforward, but its application requires careful attention to the type of act.

Guided Path
1
State the governing maxim
Identify the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' and recognize that it applies to all actions: worship and transactions.
This is a major maxim (kubra) in Hanafi legal theory.
2
Classify actions by type
Distinguish mubah, sunnah/mandub, wajib (ibadat maqsudah vs. non-maqsudah), and mu'amalat. Each has a different relation to intention.
Use the examples from the passage: eating, earning wealth, visiting the sick, praying, repaying debts, selling.
3
Apply the maxim to each category
Determine whether intention affects validity, reward, or both for each category. Mubah: no intention needed for validity or reward; sunnah/mandub: intention needed for reward, not validity; wajib (maqsudah): intention needed for validity; wajib (non-maqsudah): intention needed for reward; mu'amalat: generally no intention needed for validity.
4
Note the exception in mu'amalat
Recognize that mu'amalat are generally valid without intention, but the mudari' verb form without sawfa/sin requires intention for validity. This is a special case due to linguistic ambiguity.
This exception is a dabt (sub-maxim) of the main maxim.

Source Text

قرینہ کی وجہ سے ماضی و مضارع پر دلالت کرے تو اس سے بیع منعقد ہو جائیگی یا مصارع جو استقبال کے لئے خاص نہ ہو اس سے صحت بیچ کے لئے حال کے معنی کا ارادہ کرنا ضروری ہوتا ہے لیکن اگر کوئی قرینہ حال کے
Like eating, earning wealth, and companionship. Explanation: Mubah is every action that is permissible to do or not do, with neither preference. (Umdat al-Iydah, p. 37) The condition of permissible acts varies according to intention. For example, if there is no intention at all in earning wealth, then no reward and no sin; if the intention is to spend on sinful acts, then sin; if the intention is to spend on good deeds, then reward. Use the same analogy for eating. And since permissible acts are not among the intended acts of worship, there is no need for intention for them to be valid. However, if one intends to gain strength for good deeds or to reach good deeds through these permissible matters, then they become worship and reward will be given for them. For example, eating is a permissible matter; if strength for good deeds is intended, reward is given; if the purpose of gaining strength is for sedition and corruption, then sin; and if no intention is made, then neither sin nor reward. And masnunat and mandubat also need intention in order to be acts of obedience so that reward may be attached to them; otherwise, intention is not needed. Thus, any permissible matter becomes worship with intention. Example of masnun and mandub: like visiting the sick and offering condolences. The definition of sunnah has passed, and mandub is that in which both doing and leaving are permissible, but doing is preponderant and leaving is preponderantly abandoned. And as for wajibat: for those that are intended acts of worship, intention is necessary for their validity, like prayer, fasting, etc.; for those that are not intended acts of worship, intention is not necessary for their validity, although intention is necessary for obtaining reward, like repayment of debts and returning a deposited item, because the purpose of these and all transactions is to benefit people. And as for mu'amalat, they are of many types. Sale does not depend on intention, and similarly the annulment of sale and hire. But they said: If a contract is made with a mudari' verb that is not preceded by sawfa or sin, it depends on intention. So if one intends by it the present offer, it is a sale; otherwise not, contrary to the past tense form, because sale by it does not depend on intention. And as for the mudari' that is exclusively for the future, it is like the command; sale is not valid with it nor with intention. And we have clarified it in Sharh al-Kanz. Translation: And in any case, mu'amalat are of many types, so sale is not dependent on it, and similarly annulment and hire. But the jurists say: if a contract is made with a mudari' that does not begin with sawfa and sin, it depends on intention; and if he intends the current offer, it becomes a sale; otherwise not, contrary to the past tense form, because sale does not depend on intention and it will be a sale. And we have clarified it in Sharh al-Kanz. In any case, the mudari' that is exclusively for the future is like the command; sale is not valid with it nor with intention. (Correct) Explanation: al-Iqalah: linguistically it is lifting, and legally it is the lifting of a contract. al-Ijarah: linguistically is the sale of benefits, and legally it is a contract on benefits for compensation. al-Bay': linguistically is the transfer of property for property, and legally it is the exchange of property for property by mutual consent. Mu'amalat are the legal rulings that pertain to worldly matters and involve at least two people or two things, like sale and purchase. Imam Ibn Nujaym al-Misri says: Mu'amalat are of many types, like sale, hire, annulment, etc., but intention is not necessary for their validity; however, intention is necessary for the attachment of reward. But they said: if a contract with mudari': the contract of sale is offer and acceptance, but for the contract to be concluded, an offer and acceptance that indicate transfer of ownership in property is needed. Offer and acceptance have three forms: (1) Offer and acceptance in the past tense: in this case the sale is concluded and intention is not needed, because the past tense indicates actual occurrence, which does not require intention. (2) Offer and acceptance in a mudari' form that does not begin with sin or sawfa: since such mudari' can indicate both present and future, and for the conclusion of sale it is necessary that the offer indicates the present, so in this case intention of the present is necessary for the conclusion of sale. Imam al-Hamawi transmitted from Imam al-Tahawi that if one says 'I sell to you' or 'I buy from you' and intends the present, the sale is valid, and this is correct. From this it is known that there is a difference of opinion about using the mudari' form for offer, but the author, may Allah have mercy on him, did not mention the difference. (3) If the offer is brought in a form that is specific to the future, the sale is not concluded even if one intends the present, because intention only works when the word can bear it, i.e., it has the possibility. So if one says 'I will sell' or 'I will buy,' even with intention of present, the contract is not valid. However, if the command, which is specific to the future, indicates past or present due to a contextual clue, then sale can be concluded with it. Or if the mudari' is not specific to the future, then for the validity of sale it is necessary to intend the present meaning, but if there is a contextual clue for the present...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir explains how intention (niyyah) functions across different categories of actions. It opens by discussing mubah (permissible) acts: these do not require intention for validity, but intention determines whether they yield reward, sin, or nothing. An example is earning wealth: if one intends to spend on good deeds, it becomes worship; if on sin, sin; if no intention, no consequence. The same applies to eating. The passage then notes that sunnah and mandub acts require intention for reward, but not for validity. Wajibat are split: those that are acts of worship intended for their own sake (e.g., prayer, fasting) require intention for validity; those that are not (e.g., repaying debts) do not require intention for validity but do for reward. The bulk of the passage then turns to mu'amalat (transactions). The general rule is that contracts like sale (bay'), annulment (iqalah), and hire (ijarah) do not require intention for their validity. However, an exception is made when the contract is expressed using a present tense verb (mudari') that is not preceded by sawfa or sin. Such a verb can denote either present or future, so intention is needed to specify the present, in which case the contract is valid. If the verb is purely future (e.g., with sawfa), intention cannot make it valid. The past tense (madi) is unambiguous and needs no intention. The commentary then elaborates on the three types of offer/acceptance forms, noting a difference of opinion about the mudari' form (Imam Tahawi considered it valid with present intention) but the author (Ibn Nujaym) did not include it. The key takeaway is that intention is central to reward in all acts, but only affects legal validity in acts of worship and in exceptional transactional cases.

Key Terms
نِيَّة (niyyah): The intention in the heart that distinguishes acts of worship from habits and determines reward or sin.
مُبَاح (mubah): An action that is permissible to do or not do, with no inherent preference.
مَسْنُون (masnun): An action that is recommended, synonymous with sunnah, but not obligatory.
مَنْدُوب (mandub): An action whose performance is preponderant over leaving, but leaving is not sinful.
وَاجِب (wajib): An action that is obligatory, required to be performed, and sinful to omit.
مُعَامَلَات (mu'amalat): Worldly transactions involving exchange of property or benefits, requiring mutual consent.
صِيغَةُ الْمَاضِي (sighah al-madi): The past tense verb form used in contracts (e.g., 'ba'tu' - I sold).
الْمُضَارِعُ (al-mudari'): The present/future tense verb form (e.g., 'abi'u' - I sell/I will sell).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a sale contract concluded using the past tense form (e.g., 'ba'tu' / 'I sold')? Does it require intention (niyyah) for validity?
A) It requires intention for validity.
B) It does not require intention for validity.
C) It requires intention only if the buyer is a minor.
D) It is invalid regardless of intention.

Key Takeaway
Intention is essential for reward in all acts, but only for validity in acts of worship intended for their own sake; in transactions, intention is only needed when the contract is expressed ambiguously.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
advanced

Intention in Contracts: The Mudari' Exception
Explaining when intention affects validity and reward of actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the ikhtilaf regarding the mudari' verb form in contracts and state the Hanafi mufta bihi position on its validity with present intention.

Core Idea
A sale using the past tense needs no intention; the present tense form requires intention to specify present meaning; the future tense form cannot be salvaged by intention.

This lesson focuses on a specific furu' of the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha': the role of intention in contract formation when the verb tense is ambiguous. The passage discusses three scenarios: past tense (madi), present tense (mudari' without sawfa/sin), and future tense (mudari' with sawfa or command). You will learn the ikhtilaf about the mudari' form and the relied-upon Hanafi position as transmitted from Imam Tahawi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three verb forms for offer
Recognize the three sighas: past tense (madi), present tense without sawfa/sin (mudari' mutlaq), and future tense with sawfa or command.
Each has a different ruling on intention.
2
Apply the general rule of mu'amalat
Mu'amalat generally do not require intention for validity. However, this passage introduces an exception due to linguistic ambiguity.
The general rule is derived from the maxim and the dabt.
3
Trace the ikhtilaf
Some scholars hold that mudari' without sawfa/sin is not valid even with present intention. The passage notes that Imam Tahawi considered it valid with present intention, and this is the correct view.
The source does not detail the opposing view, but it exists.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is Imam Tahawi's: if the speaker intends present, the contract is valid. This is because the mudari' is ambiguous, and intention resolves the ambiguity.
5
Conclude the practical ruling
If someone says 'abi'u' (I sell) intending present, it is a valid sale. If no intention or future intention, the sale is not concluded. For future-specific forms (sawfa, command), even intention does not validate.

اور چونکہ اعمال مباح عبادات مقصودہ میں سے نہیں ہیں اس لئے اعمال مباح کے میچ ہونے کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے البتہ اگر ان سے نیک کاموں پر طاقت حاصل کرنے یا ان امور مباح کے ذریعہ نیک کاموں تک پہنچنے کا اگر کچھ بھی نیت نہ کی تو نہ گناہ اور نہ ثواب۔ اور مسنونات اور مندوبات بھی طاعت ہونے کے لئے تا کہ ان پر ثواب مرتب ہو سکے نیت کے محتاج ہیں ورنہ نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے لہذا نیت سے کوئی بھی امر مباح عبادت ہو جاتا ہے۔مسنون و مندوب کی مثال جیسے عیادتِ مریض اور تعزیت وغیرہ۔ سنت کی تعریف گذر چکی اور مندوب وہ ہے جس میں فعل اور ترک فعل دونوں جائز ہوں مگر فعل ( کرنا ) راج اور ترک فعل مرجوح ہو۔ اور واجبات میں پس جو عبادات مقصودہ ہیں ان کی صحت کے لئے نیت ضروری ہے جیسے نماز، روزہ وغیرہ اور جو عبادات مقصودہ نہ ہوں ان کی صحت کے لئے نیت کی ضرورت نہیں اگر چہ حصول ثواب کے لئے نیت ضروری ہے جیسے قرضوں کی ادائیگی اور نصب شدہ چیز کو واپس کرنا اس لئے کہ ان سے اور جملہ معاملات سے مقصود لوگوں کو نفع پہنچانا ہے۔ وأما المُعَامَلات فَأَنْوَاعٌ فَالبَيْعُ لا يتوقف عَلَيْهَا وَكَذَا الإقالة والأجَارَةُ لكِنْ قَالُوا إِنْ عَقَدَ بِمُضَارِعٍ لَمْ يَصْدُرْ بِسَوْفَ وَ السِّيْنِ تَوَقَّفَ عَلَى النِّيَّةِ فَإِنْ تَوَىٰ بِهِ الْإِيْجَابَ لِلْحَالِ كَانَ بَيْعاً وَإِلَّا لَا بِخِلَافِ صِيْغَةِ الْمَاضِي فَإِنَّ Like eating, earning wealth, and companionship. Explanation: Mubah is every action that is permissible to do or not do, with neither preference. (Umdat al-Iydah, p. 37) The condition of permissible acts varies according to intention. For example, if there is no intention at all in earning wealth, then no reward and no sin; if the intention is to spend on sinful acts, then sin; if the intention is to spend on good deeds, then reward. Use the same analogy for eating. And since permissible acts are not among the intended acts of worship, there is no need for intention for them to be valid. However, if one intends to gain strength for good deeds or to reach good deeds through these permissible matters, then they become worship and reward will be given for them. For example, eating is a permissible matter; if strength for good deeds is intended, reward is given; if the purpose of gaining strength is for sedition and corruption, then sin; and if no intention is made, then neither sin nor reward. And masnunat and mandubat also need intention in order to be acts of obedience so that reward may be attached to them; otherwise, intention is not needed. Thus, any permissible matter becomes worship with intention. Example of masnun and mandub: like visiting the sick and offering condolences. The definition of sunnah has passed, and mandub is that in which both doing and leaving are permissible, but doing is preponderant and leaving is preponderantly abandoned. And as for wajibat: for those that are intended acts of worship, intention is necessary for their validity, like prayer, fasting, etc.; for those that are not intended acts of worship, intention is not necessary for their validity, although intention is necessary for obtaining reward, like repayment of debts and returning a deposited item, because the purpose of these and all transactions is to benefit people. And as for mu'amalat, they are of many types. Sale does not depend on intention, and similarly the annulment of sale and hire. But they said: If a contract is made with a mudari' verb that is not preceded by sawfa or sin, it depends on intention. So if one intends by it the present offer, it is a sale; otherwise not, contrary to the past tense form, because sale by it does not depend on intention. And as for the mudari' that is exclusively for the future, it is like the command; sale is not valid with it nor with intention. And we have clarified it in Sharh al-Kanz. Translation: And in any case, mu'amalat are of many types, so sale is not dependent on it, and similarly annulment and hire. But the jurists say: if a contract is made with a mudari' that does not begin with sawfa and sin, it depends on intention; and if he intends the current offer, it becomes a sale; otherwise not, contrary to the past tense form, because sale does not depend on intention and it will be a sale. And we have clarified it in Sharh al-Kanz. In any case, the mudari' that is exclusively for the future is like the command; sale is not valid with it nor with intention. (Correct) Explanation: al-Iqalah: linguistically it is lifting, and legally it is the lifting of a contract. al-Ijarah: linguistically is the sale of benefits, and legally it is a contract on benefits for compensation. al-Bay': linguistically is the transfer of property for property, and legally it is the exchange of property for property by mutual consent. Mu'amalat are the legal rulings that pertain to worldly matters and involve at least two people or two things, like sale and purchase. Imam Ibn Nujaym al-Misri says: Mu'amalat are of many types, like sale, hire, annulment, etc., but intention is not necessary for their validity; however, intention is necessary for the attachment of reward. But they said: if a contract with mudari': the contract of sale is offer and acceptance, but for the contract to be concluded, an offer and acceptance that indicate transfer of ownership in property is needed. Offer and acceptance have three forms: (1) Offer and acceptance in the past tense: in this case the sale is concluded and intention is not needed, because the past tense indicates actual occurrence, which does not require intention. (2) Offer and acceptance in a mudari' form that does not begin with sin or sawfa: since such mudari' can indicate both present and future, and for the conclusion of sale it is necessary that the offer indicates the present, so in this case intention of the present is necessary for the conclusion of sale. Imam al-Hamawi transmitted from Imam al-Tahawi that if one says 'I sell to you' or 'I buy from you' and intends the present, the sale is valid, and this is correct. From this it is known that there is a difference of opinion about using the mudari' form for offer, but the author, may Allah have mercy on him, did not mention the difference. (3) If the offer is brought in a form that is specific to the future, the sale is not concluded even if one intends the present, because intention only works when the word can bear it, i.e., it has the possibility. So if one says 'I will sell' or 'I will buy,' even with intention of present, the contract is not valid. However, if the command, which is specific to the future, indicates past or present due to a contextual clue, then sale can be concluded with it. Or if the mudari' is not specific to the future, then for the validity of sale it is necessary to intend the present meaning, but if there is a contextual clue for the present...
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir explains how intention (niyyah) functions across different categories of actions. It opens by discussing mubah (permissible) acts: these do not require intention for validity, but intention determines whether they yield reward, sin, or nothing. An example is earning wealth: if one intends to spend on good deeds, it becomes worship; if on sin, sin; if no intention, no consequence. The same applies to eating. The passage then notes that sunnah and mandub acts require intention for reward, but not for validity. Wajibat are split: those that are acts of worship intended for their own sake (e.g., prayer, fasting) require intention for validity; those that are not (e.g., repaying debts) do not require intention for validity but do for reward. The bulk of the passage then turns to mu'amalat (transactions). The general rule is that contracts like sale (bay'), annulment (iqalah), and hire (ijarah) do not require intention for their validity. However, an exception is made when the contract is expressed using a present tense verb (mudari') that is not preceded by sawfa or sin. Such a verb can denote either present or future, so intention is needed to specify the present, in which case the contract is valid. If the verb is purely future (e.g., with sawfa), intention cannot make it valid. The past tense (madi) is unambiguous and needs no intention. The commentary then elaborates on the three types of offer/acceptance forms, noting a difference of opinion about the mudari' form (Imam Tahawi considered it valid with present intention) but the author (Ibn Nujaym) did not include it. The key takeaway is that intention is central to reward in all acts, but only affects legal validity in acts of worship and in exceptional transactional cases.

Key Terms
نِيَّة (niyyah): The intention in the heart that distinguishes acts of worship from habits and determines reward or sin.
صِيغَةُ الْمَاضِي (sighah al-madi): The past tense verb form used in contracts (e.g., 'ba'tu' - I sold).
الْمُضَارِعُ (al-mudari'): The present/future tense verb form (e.g., 'abi'u' - I sell/I will sell).
مُعَامَلَات (mu'amalat): Worldly transactions involving exchange of property or benefits, requiring mutual consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in the passage, which of the following verb forms used in a sale does NOT require intention for validity?
A) Past tense (e.g., 'ba'tu')
B) Present tense without sawfa/sin (e.g., 'abi'u')
C) Present tense with sawfa (e.g., 'sa-abi'u')
D) Command form (e.g., 'bi''ni')

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mu'amalat, the past tense is definitive; the present tense needs intention; the future tense is invalid even with intention.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
Hibah: No Intention Needed, But Consent Must Be Present
Ruling on hibah regarding intention and consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that hibah does not require intention but requires consent, and evaluate the furuat of jest, talqin, and coercion while distinguishing hibah from talaq and itaq.

Core Idea
A gift in jest is valid; a gift under duress is not. Consent, not intention, is the condition.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi contract law: the role of intention and consent in gift contracts (hibah). While many contracts require both, hibah uniquely dispenses with intention but keeps consent as an essential condition. This principle, rooted in the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions), is clarified through contrasting examples from divorce and emancipation, where even consent is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
From the passage, extract that hibah is valid without intention but requires consent. Note that the absence of intention does not invalidate hibah.
The base rule contrasts with talaq and itaq, which require neither intention nor consent.
2
Examine the furuat of jest and talqin
Analyze the two scenarios: (1) hibah in jest is valid despite lack of serious intent; (2) hibah by talqin (prompting) to someone unaware of the meaning is invalid due to missing consent.
The story of Abdullah ibn Mubarak illustrates how consent can still be present even if the request seems jesting.
3
Compare with coercion
Understand that coercion (ikrah) negates consent, rendering hibah invalid, whereas talaq and itaq under coercion are valid.
This highlights the centrality of consent for hibah.
4
Apply the qawida and its dabt
Relate the rulings to the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha'. The sub-maxim (dabt) is 'intention is not a condition in hibah'. Confirm that the rule is consistent: hibah in jest is valid because the condition is consent, not intention.
The istithna' (exception) for sale shows that sale requires both intention and consent; jest invalidates sale.

فقہاء بیان کرتے ہیں کہ گر کسی نے از راہ مذاق کوئی چیز ہبہ کر دی تو ہبہ صحیح ہو جائے گا کیونکہ از راہ مذاق ہبہ میں نیت نہیں پائی گئی لیکن صحت ہبہ کے لئے نیت ضروری نہیں ہے جیسا کہ بزازیہ میں ہے۔ ولكن لو لقن الهبة: اگر کسی نے کسی دوسرے آدمی سے کہا کہ یہ چیز تم مجھے ہبہ کردو اور وہ ہبہ کے معنی اور مطلب سے واقف نہیں اور اس نے اس کے کہنے پر وہ چیز ہبہ کر دی تو یہ ہمہ بیج نہ ہوگا صحیح نہ ہونا نیت کے مفقود ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں بلکہ صحت ہبہ کے لئے رضامندی ضروری ہے اور وہ یہاں مفقود ہے۔ ولذا لوأكرہ: جب آپ کو یہ معلوم ہو گیا کہ صحت ہبہ کے لئے رضامندی ضروری ہے تو اگر کسی سے جبر وا کراو ہبہ کرالیا گیا اور اس نے ہبہ کر دیا تو یہ ہبہ عدم رضا کی وجہ سے درست نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن یہاں ایک سوال ہوتا ہے کہ جب صحت ہبہ کے لئے رضا مندی شرط ہے اور کسی نے از راہ مذاق ہبہ کر دیاتو آپ کہتے ہیں کہ ہبہ سی ہوگیا حالانکہ از راہ مذاق ہبہ میں بھی رضا مندی نہیں پائی جاتی تو ہبہ درست نہ ہونا چاہئے تھا اور دلیل کے طور پر بزازیہ کی عبارت اور حاشیہ میں عبد اللہ بن مبارک کے واقعہ سے استدلال کیا ہے کہ ان کا گذرا ہے لوگوں کے پاس سے ہوا جو ڈھول بجا رہے تھے تو وہ وہاں ٹھہرے اور ان سے کہا کہ یہ ڈھول تم مجھے ہبہ کردو اور پھر دیکھو میں کیسے ڈھول بجاتا ہوں انہوں نے وہ ڈھول دیا عبداللہ بن مبارک نے اس کو زمین پر دے مارا وہ پاش پاش ہو گیا۔ ان کا جواب یہ دیا گیا ہے کہ یہاں مزاح اور مذاق طلب ہبہ میں ہے اور ہبہ میں مزاح و مذاق نہیں ہے اس لئے یہاں بھی سید رضا مندی کی وجہ سے بیج اور درست ہو جائے گا۔ رہا یہ مسئلہ کہ عبد اللہ بن مبارک نے ہبہ کے ہدیہ کے طور پر لے کر ڈھول کو توڑ دیا اور پاش پاش کر دیا علماء نے اس کی وجہ یہ لکھی ہے کہ لوگوں کو منکرات سے روکنا تو بقدر استطاعت ضروری ہے لیکن امام ابو حنیفہ کا مسلک یہ ہے کہ آلات لہو واجب کسی دوسرے کے اس کی اجازت کے بغیر توڑنا درست نہیں ہے اور اگر بدون اجازت کسی دوسرے کے ابو احب کے آلات کو پاش پاش کر دیا تو ضمان واجب ہوگا ۔ عبد اللہ بن مبارک علیہ الرحمہ نے اس لئے آلات لہو ولعب کو بطور ہدلیا تاکہ ان کا اپنی ہی ملکیت میں توڑ نا لازم آئے اور ضمان دینا نہ پڑے۔ بخلاف الطلاق والعتاق : اگر کسی نے طلاق اور اعتماق کے الفاظ کی تلقین کی اور جس کو تلقین کی گئی ہے؟" ان کے معنی و مطلب نہ سمجھتا ہوں اور اس کی تلقین سے کسی نے یہ الفاظ طلاق و اعتاق اپنی زبان سے نکالدئے تو طلاق وعناق کا تحقق ہو جائے گا کیونکہ ان کے وقوع کے لئے رضا مندی شرط نہیں ہے۔ ولذا لو اكرہ: جب آپ کو یہ معلوم ہو گیا وقوع طلاق وعتاق میں رضامندی شرط نہیں ہے۔ اگر کس نے زبردنی
As for the gift, it does not depend on intention. They said: If one gives a gift jestingly, it is valid, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. But if one prompts the gift and he does not know it, it is not valid, not because intention is a condition, but only due to the loss of its condition, which is consent. Similarly, if one is coerced upon it, it is not valid. Contrary to divorce and emancipation, for they both occur by prompting from one who does not know them, because consent is not a condition for them. Similarly, if one is coerced upon them, they occur. ...
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) on the gift contract (hibah). It clarifies that unlike sale, hibah does not require intention (niyyah) for its validity. Therefore, if someone gives a gift in jest, it is valid because the absence of intention does not affect it. However, hibah requires the consent (rida) of the donor. Hence, if someone prompts a person who does not understand the meaning of hibah, and that person gives a gift without understanding, it is invalid due to lack of consent. Similarly, a gift under duress is invalid. In contrast, divorce (talaq) and emancipation (itaq) are valid even without consent, so they take effect even if the person is prompted or coerced. The author supports the ruling on hibah in jest with a story of Abdullah ibn Mubarak, who asked for a drum as a gift from people playing it, then broke it. This shows that the gift was taken with serious intent (to destroy a musical instrument) even though the request was made in a jesting manner, thus consent was present. The passage also notes that in the Hanafi school, breaking others' musical instruments without permission is not allowed and incurs liability (daman). Abdullah ibn Mubarak therefore took the drum as a gift so that he could break it without liability. This illustrates a legal stratagem (hila) to avoid guarantee while fulfilling the duty of forbidding evil.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): Gift; transfer of ownership without compensation
رضا (rida): Consent of the donor
تلقين (talqin): Prompting someone to utter a legal statement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone prompts another to give a gift, and the person does not understand what 'hibah' means but still gives the item, is the gift valid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Yes, because intention is not required
B) No, because there is no proper acceptance
C) No, because consent is lacking
D) Yes, if the item is valuable

Key Takeaway
Hibah requires consent, not intention; therefore, a gift in jest is valid, but a coerced gift or one given by an unaware donor is invalid, unlike talaq and itaq where consent is irrelevant.

Hiba Exception to Intentions Maxim
Explaining the exception to the maxim 'actions by intentions' for hiba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the exception to the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' by identifying when a gift (hiba) is valid despite lack of intention, and distinguish between absence of intention and absence of consent.

Core Idea
A gift given in jest is valid because hiba does not require intention; consent suffices.

The major fiqh maxim 'Actions are judged by intentions' governs most contracts, but there is a well-known exception: the gift (hiba) does not require intention for its validity. This lesson examines that exception, its basis in Hanafi usul, and its application in cases of jesting gifts. It also explores a related legal stratagem (hila) used by Abdullah ibn Mubarak to break musical instruments without incurring liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its exception
State the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' and note that hiba is an exception as intention is not a condition.
The dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) is 'الهبة لا تشترط فيها النية'.
2
Analyze the furu' of a jesting gift
Consider the ruling: if someone gives a gift jokingly, it is valid because intention is not required. However, consent (rida) must be present.
The jest is in the request, not in the act of giving, so consent is present.
3
Address the contradiction about consent
The objection: jesting lacks consent, so the gift should be invalid. The answer: the jest is only in the request; the giver sincerely consents to the transfer.
Contrast with compulsion (ikrah), where consent is absent and the gift is invalid.
4
Apply to the Abdullah ibn Mubarak story
Abdullah ibn Mubarak requested a drum as a gift (jestingly), obtained valid ownership, then broke it. This illustrates the exception and a lawful stratagem to avoid liability.
The breaking of another's property without permission incurs daman; he used hiba to become owner.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that intention is not a condition for hiba, but consent is required. This is supported by Bazzaziyya and other later texts.
This is the operative position for fatwa.

Source Text

ہے جیسا کہ بزازیہ کے حوالہ سے معلوم ہوتا ہے اور علامہ حموی نے خلاصہ کے حوالہ سے بھی یہی حل کیا ہے۔ قالوا لو وهب ممازحا بہبہ میں چونکہ نیت ضروری نہیں ہے اس وجہ سے فقہاء بیان کرتے ہیں کہ گر کسی نے از راہ مذاق کوئی چیز ہبہ کر دی تو ہبہ صحیح ہو جائے گا کیونکہ از راہ مذاق ہبہ میں نیت نہیں پائی گئی لیکن صحت ہبہ کے لئے نیت ضروری نہیں ہے جیسا کہ بزازیہ میں ہے۔ ولكن لو لقن الهبة: اگر کسی نے کسی دوسرے آدمی سے کہا کہ یہ چیز تم مجھے ہبہ کردو اور وہ ہبہ کے معنی اور مطلب سے واقف نہیں اور اس نے اس کے کہنے پر وہ چیز ہبہ کر دی تو یہ ہمہ بیج نہ ہوگا صحیح نہ ہونا نیت کے مفقود ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں بلکہ صحت ہبہ کے لئے رضامندی ضروری ہے اور وہ یہاں مفقود ہے۔ ولذا لوأكرہ: جب آپ کو یہ معلوم ہو گیا کہ صحت ہبہ کے لئے رضامندی ضروری ہے تو اگر کسی سے جبر وا کراو ہبہ کرالیا گیا اور اس نے ہبہ کر دیا تو یہ ہبہ عدم رضا کی وجہ سے درست نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن یہاں ایک سوال ہوتا ہے کہ جب صحت ہبہ کے لئے رضا مندی شرط ہے اور کسی نے از راہ مذاق ہبہ کر دیاتو آپ کہتے ہیں کہ ہبہ سی ہوگیا حالانکہ از راہ مذاق ہبہ میں بھی رضا مندی نہیں پائی جاتی تو ہبہ درست نہ ہونا چاہئے تھا اور دلیل کے طور پر بزازیہ کی عبارت اور حاشیہ میں عبد اللہ بن مبارک کے واقعہ سے استدلال کیا ہے کہ ان کا گذرا ہے لوگوں کے پاس سے ہوا جو ڈھول بجا رہے تھے تو وہ وہاں ٹھہرے اور ان سے کہا کہ یہ ڈھول تم مجھے ہبہ کردو اور پھر دیکھو میں کیسے ڈھول بجاتا ہوں انہوں نے وہ ڈھول دیا عبداللہ بن مبارک نے اس کو زمین پر دے مارا وہ پاش پاش ہو گیا۔ ان کا جواب یہ دیا گیا ہے کہ یہاں مزاح اور مذاق طلب ہبہ میں ہے اور ہبہ میں مزاح و مذاق نہیں ہے اس لئے یہاں بھی سید رضا مندی کی وجہ سے بیج اور درست ہو جائے گا۔ رہا یہ مسئلہ کہ عبد اللہ بن مبارک نے ہبہ کے ہدیہ کے طور پر لے کر ڈھول کو توڑ دیا اور پاش پاش کر دیا علماء نے اس کی وجہ یہ لکھی ہے کہ لوگوں کو منکرات سے روکنا تو بقدر استطاعت ضروری ہے لیکن امام ابو حنیفہ کا مسلک یہ ہے کہ آلات لہو واجب کسی دوسرے کے اس کی اجازت کے بغیر توڑنا درست نہیں ہے اور اگر بدون اجازت کسی دوسرے کے ابو احب کے آلات کو پاش پاش کر دیا تو ضمان واجب ہوگا ۔ عبد اللہ بن مبارک علیہ الرحمہ نے اس لئے آلات لہو ولعب کو بطور ہدلیا تاکہ ان کا اپنی ہی ملکیت میں توڑ نا لازم آئے اور ضمان دینا نہ پڑے۔
As is known from the reference of Bazzaziyya, and Allamah Hamawi also solved it the same way from the reference of Khulasa. They said: If one gives a gift jokingly, because intention is not necessary in hiba, therefore the jurists state that if someone gives something as a gift jokingly, the gift will be valid, because in a joking gift, intention is not found, but intention is not necessary for the validity of a gift, as in Bazzaziyya. But if someone prompted the gift: If someone told another person, 'Give me this thing as a gift,' and he is not aware of the meaning and import of gift, and he gave the thing at his saying, then this gift will not be valid. Not being valid is not because of the absence of intention, but because for the validity of gift, consent is necessary, and here it is absent. Therefore if someone was compelled: When you know that for validity of gift consent is necessary, then if someone was forced and compelled to give a gift and he gave it, this gift will not be valid due to lack of consent. But here a question arises: When for validity of gift consent is a condition and someone gave a gift jokingly, you say that the gift is valid, although in a joking gift too consent is not found, so the gift should not have been valid. And as evidence, the passage from Bazzaziyya and the story of Abdullah ibn Mubarak from the marginalia are cited: He passed by people who were playing drums, so he stopped and told them, 'Give me this drum as a gift, and then see how I play it.' They gave the drum. Abdullah ibn Mubarak struck it on the ground and it shattered into pieces. The answer given is that here the jest and joking are in requesting the gift, and in the gift itself there is no jest and joking, therefore here also the gift becomes valid due to sincere consent. As for the issue that Abdullah ibn Mubarak took the drum as a gift and broke it and shattered it, scholars have written its reason: Preventing people from forbidden things is necessary as much as possible, but the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa is that breaking musical instruments of another without his permission is not permissible, and if one breaks another's property without permission, compensation becomes obligatory. Abdullah ibn Mubarak (may Allah have mercy on him) therefore took the musical instruments as a gift so that breaking his own property becomes necessary and he does not have to pay compensation.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents a specific application of the major qawida 'الأمور بمقاصدها' (Actions are judged by intentions). The rule is that hiba (gift) is an exception: its validity does not require intention (niyyah). Therefore, even if someone gives a gift jokingly without actually intending to give it away, the gift is valid according to the Hanafi school. However, consent (rida) is required; if the gift is given under compulsion (ikrah), it is not valid. The text then addresses a potential objection: if joking lacks consent, why is a joking gift valid? The answer is that the jest is in the request, not in the act of giving; the giver consents to the request even if the request is jesting. The story of Abdullah ibn Mubarak illustrates a legal stratagem: he wanted to break the drums (musical instruments) to stop a wrong, but Abu Hanifa's position is that breaking another's property without permission incurs liability (daman). So he first obtained the drum as a gift, making himself the owner, then broke it without liability. This shows the practical application of hiba rulings and the principle of using lawful means to achieve a praiseworthy end.

Key Terms
هبة (hiba): A gift; a voluntary transfer of ownership without consideration
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart to perform the act for a specific purpose
رضا (rida): Consent; willingness to perform the act

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a gift given jokingly without intention?
A) It is invalid because intention is required for all contracts.
B) It is valid because hiba does not require intention.
C) It is invalid because consent is absent.
D) It is valid only if the gift is of minimal value.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'actions by intentions' has a well-known exception: a gift (hiba) does not require intention for validity; consent suffices, making even a jesting gift valid.

Exceptions to the Sarih Talaq Rule: Intention and Context
Three exceptions to the sarih talaq rule with qada/diyanah distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that sarih talaq does not require niyyah and identify three cases where context overrides the apparent meaning, applying the distinction between qada' and diyanah.

Core Idea
Explicit divorce words normally need no intention, but clear context of non-marital intent can block talaq diyanatan while still requiring it qada'an.

In Hanafi fiqh, a key principle is that explicit divorce words (sarih) take effect without the need for the husband's inner intention (niyyah). However, this principle is not absolute. Our passage presents three scenarios where, due to clear contextual evidence (qarinah) that the speaker did not mean marital divorce, the ruling differs between God's judgment (diyanah) and the court's judgment (qada'). Understanding these exceptions is essential for the mufti to give correct fatwa and for the judge to rule justly.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the General Rule
The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that sarih talaq (clear divorce words like 'You are divorced') takes effect immediately without requiring the husband's intention. This is because the words themselves are unambiguous indicators of divorce.
This rule is based on the principle that explicit expressions are self-interpreting.
2
Identify the Three Exception Cases
The passage lists three situations: (1) repeating divorce phrases in the wife's presence without intent, (2) writing divorce and then reading it to the wife at her request, and (3) saying 'You are divorced' while intending to untie a rope. In each, the contextual evidence (qarinah) shows the husband did not intend marital divorce.
These are not contradictions but clarifications of the rule's scope.
3
Distinguish Between Diyanah and Qada'
In the third case (rope), the ruling splits: diyanah (before God) considers the true intention, so no divorce occurs; qada' (court) looks at the apparent words, so divorce is enforced. This dual ruling protects the wife's rights while acknowledging the husband's inner state.
The mufti must explain both aspects to the public.
4
Understand the Istithna' (Exception) to the Rule
The author notes that these cases do not negate the rule that sarih does not need intention. Rather, the presence of a strong contrary indication (qarinah) overrides the apparent meaning, creating an exception (istithna'). This is a common pattern in Hanafi usul: rules have exceptions when context changes the operative meaning.
This is an example of the maxim 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' applied to divorce.
5
Apply the Principle to New Nawazil
When a mufti encounters a case where the husband uses explicit divorce words but claims a different intention, he must examine the contextual evidence. If there is a strong, credible qarinah indicating non-marital intent (e.g., a joke, a slip, or a reference to an object), the ruling may follow the exceptions here.
However, the default remains that sarih words effect talaq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالُوْالَوْ كَرَّرَ مَسَائِلَ الطَّلَاقِ بِحَضْرَتِهَا وَيَقُوْلُ فِي كُلِّ مَرَّةٍ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ لَمْ يَقَعُ وَلَوْ كَتَبَتْ إِمْرَأَتِي طَالِقٌ أَوْ أَنْتِ طَالِقَ وَقَالَتْ لَهُ إِقْرَأ عَلَيَّ فَقَرَا عَلَيْهَا لِعَدَمِ قَصْدِه بالـ وَلَا يُنَا فِيْهِ قَوْ لُهُمْ إِنَّ الصَّرِيحَ لَا يَحْتَاجُ إلى النِّيَّةِ وَقَالُوْا لَوْ قَالَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ نَاوِياً الطَّلَاقِ مِنْ وَثَاقِ لَمْ يَقَعُ دِيانة ووقع قضاءً.
They said: If he repeated issues of divorce in her presence and said each time 'You are divorced', it does not occur. And if he wrote 'My wife is divorced' or 'You are divorced' and she said to him 'Read it to me', and he read it to her, due to lack of intention... And their statement that the explicit does not need intention does not contradict this. And they said: If he said 'You are divorced' intending divorce from a rope (i.e., to untie her from a rope), it does not occur in diyanah but occurs in qada.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents three specific cases where talaq does not occur despite the use of explicit words, due to lack of intention or a clear alternative meaning. First: repeating divorce phrases in the wife's presence without actual intention—the repetition is not considered real divorce. Second: writing divorce and then reading it to the wife at her request—since he is just reciting, not intending, it does not effect divorce. The author notes that this does not contradict the rule that explicit words do not need intention, because in these cases a counter-indication (qarinah) shows the lack of intent. Third: if someone says 'You are divorced' but intends to divorce a rope (i.e., to untie a knot), in diyanah it is not divorce, but in qada the judge rules divorce because the apparent meaning is talaq. These cases illustrate the nuanced application of the sarih rule: while explicit words generally effect talaq without intention, when there is clear evidence that the speaker did not mean marital divorce, the ruling differs between qada and diyanah.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Clear, explicit words of divorce that are unambiguous in meaning, e.g., 'You are divorced'.
ديانة (diyanatan): The internal, religious dimension of the ruling based on true intention, before God.
قضاء (qada'an): The external, court-enforced ruling based on apparent words and circumstances.
قرينة (qarinah): Contextual evidence or indication that clarifies the speaker's true intent.
وثاق (withaq): A rope or shackle; in the text, referring to a physical binding from which divorce is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the diyanah ruling if a husband says 'You are divorced' but intends to divorce a rope?
A) Talaq occurs
B) Talaq does not occur
C) Talaq occurs with the wife
D) The marriage is annulled

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that sarih talaq does not need intention has exceptions when a strong contextual indication (qarinah) shows the husband did not mean marital divorce, leading to a split between diyanah and qada' in the rope case.

Intention of Number of Talaq in Explicit Phrases
Explains valid intentions for number of talaq in explicit phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that in explicit divorce phrases, intention of number is limited by linguistic form, and identify the mufta bihi ruling for 'anti taliq' and 'anti al-talaq'.

Core Idea
The linguistic form of the divorce phrase determines the validity of intended number: sarih adjective limits to one, masdar allows three.

This lesson covers a key rasm al-mufti rule: the effect of the husband's intention when using explicit divorce phrases. In Hanafi fatwa, the linguistic nature of the expression—whether it is an adjective like 'taliq' or a verbal noun like 'talaq'—determines how many divorces can be intended. The rule ensures that the husband cannot use a single utterance to produce more divorces than the phrase can bear. Understanding this distinction is crucial for muftis when issuing fatwas on talaq.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between sarih and kinayah phrases
Explicit (sarih) divorce phrases are those that clearly indicate divorce, like 'anti taliq' (you are divorced). Implicit (kinayah) phrases require intention. Here we are dealing with explicit phrases.
2
Identify the linguistic form of 'anti taliq'
'Taliq' is an adjective (sifah) describing the wife, singular feminine. Its form can only bear one divorce.
Contrast with 'anti al-talaq' where 'talaq' is a masdar (verbal noun).
3
Apply the rule: intention of three or ba'in is invalid in 'anti taliq'
The husband's intention cannot override the linguistic capacity of the word. Only one revocable divorce occurs, regardless of intention.
This is the mufta bihi position based on Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Apply the rule for 'anti al-talaq': intention of three valid, intention of two invalid (except for slave woman)
Masdar allows more flexibility: intention of three is accepted because the word is not tied to singular form. Intention of two fails because linguistic usage does not support it for free women.
The exception for slave women is based on their legal capacity for two divorces at once in some contexts.
5
Formulate the fatwa conclusion
For a husband who says 'anti taliq' intending three, the fatwa is: one revocable divorce. For 'anti al-talaq' intending three, three divorces occur. For 'anti al-talaq' intending two, only one (or two if slave).
Always check status of wife (free/slave, consummated) as it affects the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And they said: The intention of three (divorces) is not valid in 'anti taliq', nor the intention of ba'in (irrevocable divorce), and the intention of two is not valid in the verbal noun 'anti al-talaq' unless the woman is a slave woman, and the intention of three is valid. Translation: The jurists say that in 'anti taliq' the intention of three and the intention of ba'in are not valid, and in the verbal noun 'anti al-talaq' the intention of two is not valid except if the woman is a slave woman, and the intention of three is valid. Explanation: If someone said 'anti taliq' and his wife is a consummated wife, then in this case one revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs, even if he did not intend it. If the husband intended three or a ba'in divorce, the intention of three or ba'in will not be valid. The reason is that by intending ba'in, the husband intended to immediately effect what is suspended upon completion of the 'iddah; therefore his own decision is returned upon him and only one revocable divorce occurs. In 'anti taliq', according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the intention of three is not valid because 'taliq' is an adjective of the woman, which is singular...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents a specific rule about the effect of intention on the number of divorces when using explicit divorce phrases. When a husband says 'anti taliq' (you are divorced), if no intention is mentioned, one revocable divorce occurs. If he intends three divorces or an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce, such intention is invalid and only one revocable divorce results. The reason given is that the word 'taliq' is an adjective describing the woman, and its singular form cannot bear the meaning of multiple divorces. However, if he says 'anti al-talaq' (you are the divorce), which is a verbal noun (masdar), he can validly intend three divorces, but not two (unless the wife is a slave woman, in which case two is possible). This distinction shows that the form of the expression limits the possible intentions. The passage emphasizes that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the authoritative transmitted rulings, the intention of three in 'anti taliq' is not accepted. This is a practical rule in Hanafi fatwa: a husband cannot use 'anti taliq' to effect three divorces at once by mere intention.

Key Terms
انت طالق (anti taliq): An explicit divorce phrase meaning 'you are divorced' - results in one revocable divorce unless wife is a slave or pregnant etc.
انت الطلاق (anti al-talaq): A masdar phrase meaning 'you are the divorce' - can carry intention of three, but not two (except for a slave woman).
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The authoritative transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Three divorces occur because intention is valid.
B) One revocable divorce occurs; the intention of three is invalid.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs.
D) The divorce is suspended until the intention is clarified.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa, the linguistic form of an explicit divorce phrase limits the valid intention for number: 'anti taliq' yields one revocable; 'anti al-talaq' allows three but not two (except for slave).

Kinayah in Talaq: When Context or Custom Replaces Intention
Explaining the rule of kinayah words with context and custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-thabit bi al-urf ka al-thabit bi al-nass' to determine when a kinayah word becomes explicit in talaq and state the mufta bihi ruling for qada' and diyanah.

Core Idea
For kinayah words, context or custom can substitute for intention in qada', but intention still matters diyanatan.

In Hanafi law, kinayah (allusive) words for divorce require either explicit intention or a clear contextual indication. This lesson explores how the discussion of divorce (mudhakarah al-talaq) or established custom can make a kinayah word function like an explicit (sarih) word in court, while the inner intention remains decisive between the husband and Allah. The underlying maxim is the wusta (intermediate) qawida: 'What is established by custom is like what is established by a textual proof.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the two planes of judgment
Distinguish qada' (court ruling based on appearances) from diyanah (the inner truth between the person and Allah). The passage shows that for kinayah words, these two planes may yield different results based on intention and context.
Qada' is bound by what is apparent, while diyanah considers the actual intention.
2
Analyse the role of context (qarina)
When a husband says 'adhhabi' during a dispute about talaq, the context is a strong indication of divorce intent. This context stands in place of explicit intention for qada', so the divorce is enforced even if the husband denies intent.
Context alone is not enough for diyanah; intention is required there.
3
Examine the exception for the word 'haram'
Although 'haram' is technically kinayah, it has become customarily explicit for divorce, so no intention is needed for the divorce itself. However, if a specific type like ba'in is intended, intention is required for that aspect.
This shows how custom (urf) elevates a word from kinayah to sarih status.
4
Apply the maxim (qawida) to new cases
The maxim 'al-thabit bi al-urf ka al-thabit bi al-nass' means that a well-established custom gives a word the legal force of an explicit text. Use this to assess whether a particular kinayah word in a specific community or context should be treated as sarih.
Custom must be general (not individual) to have this effect.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The passage confirms that the relied-upon position is: qada'an, divorce occurs with context or custom; diyanatan, intention is required. For fatwa, muftis must consider both the contextual factors and the husband's inner state when advising.
The later Hanafi masters (mutakhkhirin) have settled on this distinction.

Whether there is discussion of divorce or not. If someone said to his wife 'adhhabi' and before that there was a dispute between them, then in that situation, qada' decrees the occurrence of divorce even if the husband denies intention. But the intention of divorce is only valid when the husband says 'I intended divorce by adhhabi', and if he did not intend, then diyanatan the ruling is non-occurrence. Qada'an, from kinayah words, divorce is intended when he intends divorce or there is a clear context (qarina) like he explicitly says 'I intended divorce by adhhabi' or there was a quarrel and discussion of divorce, then the husband says 'adhhabi' - qada'an divorce occurs even if he denies intention because qada' goes by appearances and this contextual indication shows the meaning of divorce. Diyanatan, for occurrence of divorce intention is necessary, whether there is discussion of divorce or not, both are equal. However, the word 'haram' although from kinayah, is explicit in the meaning of divorce, so immediate intention is not needed for divorce. Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a qawida with furuat explaining the ruling of kinayah words in talaq and the role of intention, context (qarina), and custom. The author first discusses the example of the word 'adhhabi'. If a husband says this to his wife during a dispute, qada'an (in court) divorce occurs even if he denies intention, because the context of dispute indicates divorce. However, diyanatan (according to conscience and between him and Allah), the ruling depends on his actual intention. The general rule for kinayah words is that qada'an, divorce requires either explicit intention or a clear context (qarina) that indicates divorce. The discussion of talaq (mudhakarah al-talaq) is considered a qarina that stands in place of intention. Then an exception is made for the word 'haram': although it is kinayah, it has become customarily explicit for divorce, so intention is not needed for divorce itself. However, if one intends talaq ba'in by it, then intention is needed for the ba'in nature because that aspect is based on intention. Finally, the author states the general principle: if a kinayah word is considered explicit by the custom of a particular people or place, then custom is authoritative, and intention is not needed. This is based on the maxim 'al-thabit bi al-urf ka al-thabit bi al-nass' (what is established by custom is like what is established by a textual proof). This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim under the kubra maxim 'al-‘adah muhakkamah' (custom is authoritative). The istithna' (exception) is that for the word 'haram', if the intended divorce is ba'in, then intention is needed for the ba'in aspect, as per the maxim 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions).

Key Terms
نیت (niyyah): Intention of the husband to effect divorce when using kinayah words
قرینہ (qarina): Contextual indication; here specifically the discussion of talaq (mudhakarah) which substitutes for explicit intention
عرف (urf): Custom of a people or place that determines the meaning of words
کنائی (kinayah): Allusive words that are not explicit for talaq and require intention or context
صریح (sarih): Explicit words that effect talaq without need for intention

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is sarih in all aspects, no intention needed.
D) It is kinayah and requires a qarina.

Key Takeaway
For kinayah words in talaq, custom and context can substitute for intention in qada' rulings, but diyanatan intention remains decisive.

Explicit and Kinayah Words in Five Marital Actions
Applying explicit/kinayah rule to five marital actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify explicit and kinayah words in tafwid, khul', ila', zihar, and ruju' and apply the Hanafi rule that intention is required only for kinayah.

Core Idea
Explicit words need no intention; kinayah words need intention across five marital actions.

This passage extends the explicit/kinayah distinction beyond divorce to four other marital actions and to ruju'. The same principle applies consistently: if the wording is clear and unambiguous, a judge does not investigate intention; if the wording is allusive, the effect depends on the speaker's intent. The passage provides concrete examples from Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five actions covered
The passage lists tafwid al-talaq (delegation of divorce), khul' (divorce for compensation), ila' (oath of abstinence), zihar (injurious assimilation), and ruju' (taking back the wife during 'iddah).
2
Recognize explicit words for each action
For each action, the passage gives examples of explicit words that clearly indicate the action without ambiguity. For tafwid, 'fawwadtu ilayka' is explicit; for khul', 'khala'tuka 'ala kaza' (with compensation); for ila', a clear oath; for zihar, 'anti 'alayya ka-zahri ummi'; for ruju', 'raja'tuki'.
Explicit words are those that have no other plausible meaning.
3
Recognize kinayah words for each action
Kinayah words are ambiguous and may refer to something else. For tafwid, 'ikhtari' or 'amruki bi-yadiki'; for khul', 'khala'tuki' or 'bara'tuki' without compensation; for ila', 'la atitu ma'aki 'ala firash'; for zihar, 'anti mithlu ummi'; for ruju', general expressions of affection.
Kinayah words require the speaker's intention to achieve legal effect.
4
Apply the rule: explicit vs kinayah
The rule from the passage: what is explicit does not require intention (qada'an), and what is kinayah requires intention. This means that if the words are explicit, a judge will enforce the action without asking the husband's intent; if kinayah, the judge will ask if he intended the action.
The ruling is from Ibn Nujaym and is consistent across all five actions.
5
Apply the analogy for ruju'
Ruju' is treated like marriage (nikah) because it continues the existing marital bond. Explicit words (like 'raja'tuki') need no intention; kinayah words (like 'anta 'indi' with context) need intention. This is a further application of the same principle.
The passage explicitly draws this analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا تَفْوِيْضُ الطَّلَاقِ وَالْخَلْعُ وَالإيْلاءُ والظَّهَارُ فَمَا كَانَ مِنْهُ صَرِيْحاً لَا تُشْتَرَطَ لَهُ النِّيَّةُ وَمَا كَانَ كِنَايَةً أُشْتَرِطَتْ لَهُ.
ترجمـ
اور بہر حال تفویض طلاق خلع ، ایلاء، اور ظہار سوجو ان میں سے صریح ہو اس کے لئے نیت شرط نہیں اور جو کنا یہ ہو اس کے لئے نیت شرط ہے۔
تشریح تفویض طلاق شوہر اپنی بیوی کی طلاق کے حق کو کسی دوسرے آدمی کو سونپ دے یا اپنی بیوی ہی کے پرد کرے۔ جیسے شوہر بیوی سے کہدے اختاری او امرک بیدک ۔ یاکسی دوسرے سے کہدے فوضت البک طلاق امرأتی
خلع: شوہر اپنی بیوی سے مال پر ضلع کرلے مثلا شوہر بیوی سے کہے خالـعتك على كذا اور عورت قبول کرلے ایلاء یہ ہے کہ شوہر اپنی بیوی کے پاس چار ماہ یا اس سے زائد نہ جانے کی قسم کھالے جیسے لا اجـامـعک الی اربعة شهر هو عبارة عن اليمين على ترك وطئ المنكوحة اربعة اشهر او اكثر هذا في اصطلاح الفقهاء والمعنى اللغوى الايلاء بالمد الحلف -
ظهار الظهار والتظهر والتظاهر عبارة عن قول الرجل لامرأته انت على كظهر أمى مشتق من الظهر و خصوا الظهر دون غيره لأنه موضع الركوب والمرأة مركوبة اذا تمشيت واصطلاحا عرفه الحنفية بانه تشبيه المسلم روضة او جزء تسائعاً فها بمحرم علیه تابیداً (شامی ج ۵ ص /۳۳) تفویض طلاق میں الفاظ کنائی کی مثال ان يقول الزوج لزوجته اختاری او امرک بیدک اور ضلع میں خالعتک او بار أتک ان دونوں میں عوض کا تذکرہ نہیں اس لئے یہ کنائی ہیں اور ایلاء میں الفاظ کنائی لا اتيت معك على فراش اولا اقرب فراشک او لا تجمع رأسي ورأسك وسادة - اور اظہار میں انت مثل امی او ،کامی فكما نوى لانه من الكنايات تفویض طلاق، خلع ، ایلاء، اور ظہار کے بارے میں علامہ ابن نجیم المصری فرماتے ہیں کہ جو الفاظ ان کے لئے مرتع ہیں ان میں قضاء نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اور جو ان میں کنائی الفاظ ہیں ان کی صحت کے لئے قضاء نیت ضروری ہے۔
وَأَمَّا الرَّجْعَةُ فَكَالنِّكَاحِ لأَنَّهَا إِسْتِدَامَتُهُ لَكِنْ مَا كَانَ مِنْهَا صَرِيْحاً لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهَا وكتابتها تحتاج إلَيْهَا.
ترجمہ: اور رہی رجعت وہ نکاح کی طرح ہے اس لئے کہ وہ اس کی بقا کو طلب کرنا ہے لیکن اس میں سے جو رتا ہیں ان میں نیت کی حاجت نہیں اور جو ان میں کنائی ہیں ان میں نسیت لابدی ہے۔ تشريح: الرجعة لغة هي مصدر رجعه يرجعه رجعاً ورجعة وهي بمعنى الرد والاعادة اصطلاحاً عرفها الحنفية بانها استدامة الملك القائم فى العدة برد الزوجة الى زوجها
As for tafwid al-talaq, khul', ila', and zihar - what from them is explicit, intention is not required for it, and what is kinayah, intention is required for it. Translation: And in any case, tafwid talaq, khul', ila', and zihar - what is explicit among them, intention is not a condition, and what is kinayah, intention is a condition. Explanation: Tafwid al-talaq: the husband delegates the right of divorce to another person or to his wife herself. For example, the husband says to his wife 'ikhtari' or 'amruki bi-yadiki', or says to another 'fawwadtu ilayka talaqa imra'ati'. Khul': the husband divorces his wife in exchange for wealth, e.g., the husband says 'khala'tuka 'ala kadha' and the woman accepts. Ila': the husband swears not to approach his wife for four months or more, e.g., 'la ajami'uki ila arba'ati ashhur'. It is an oath to abandon intercourse with the wife for four months or more; this is in the terminology of jurists, and linguistically ila' means swearing. Zihar: az-zihar, at-tazahhur, and at-tazahhur is the husband's saying to his wife 'anti 'alayya ka-zahri ummi', derived from 'zahr'; they specified the back over other body parts because it is the place of riding, and the woman is ridden (in intercourse). In terminology, the Hanafis define it as likening the Muslim wife or part of her to a permanently forbidden relative. (Shami, vol. 5, p. 33). In tafwid al-talaq, examples of kinayah words are when the husband says to his wife 'ikhtari' or 'amruki bi-yadiki'. In khul', 'khala'tuki' or 'bara'tuki' without mention of compensation, so they are kinayah. In ila', kinayah words are 'la atitu ma'aki 'ala firash' or 'la aqrabu firashaki' or 'la ajma'u ra'si wa ra'saki wisadatan'. In zihar, 'anti mithlu ummi' or 'kami' - so he must intend because it is from kinayah. Regarding tafwid talaq, khul', ila', and zihar, Allamah Ibn Nujaym al-Misri says: the words that are explicit for them, intention is not required qada'an, and those that are kinayah, intention is required qada'an. As for ruju' (taking back the wife), it is like marriage because it is the continuation of it. But what from it is explicit does not need it (intention), and what is kinayah needs it. Translation: And as for ruju', it is like marriage because it seeks its continuity. But what from it is explicit does not need intention, and what is kinayah, intention is necessary. Explanation: Ruju' linguistically means return; terminologically the Hanafis define it as the continuation of existing marital authority during the 'iddah by returning the wife to the husband.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage applies the same distinction between explicit (sarih) and allusive (kinayah) words to four other types of marital actions: tafwid al-talaq (delegation of divorce), khul' (divorce for compensation), ila' (vow of abstinence), and zihar (injurious assimilation). For each, if the words used are explicit (e.g., in tafwid, 'fawwadtu ilayka'; in khul', 'khala'tuka 'ala kaza'; in ila', explicit oath; in zihar, 'anti 'alayya ka-zahri ummi'), no intention is required qada'an. If kinayah words are used (e.g., in tafwid, 'ikhtari' or 'amruki bi-yadiki'; in khul', 'khala'tuki' without mention of compensation; in ila', 'la atitu ma'aki 'ala firash'; in zihar, 'anti mithlu ummi'), then intention is required. The author cites Allamah Ibn Nujaym for this rule. Then the same principle is applied to ruju' (taking back the wife during 'iddah). Ruju' is akin to marriage as it continues the existing bond. Explicit words for ruju' (like 'raja'tuki') need no intention, while kinayah words (like 'anta 'indi' with intention) do require intention. The Hanafi definition of ruju' is provided: the continuation of existing marital authority during the 'iddah by returning the wife.

Key Terms
تفویض طلاق (tafwid al-talaq): Delegation of the husband's right to divorce to another person or to the wife herself
خلع (khul'): Divorce in exchange for compensation given by the wife, with the husband's acceptance
ایلاء (ila'): An oath taken by the husband to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife for four months or more
ظہار (zihar): A husband saying to his wife that she is like the back of his mother, a pre-Islamic form of divorce; requires expiation
رجعت (ruju'): The act of taking back the wife during her 'iddah after a revocable divorce, thereby continuing the marriage

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For which of the following actions is intention (niyyah) required qada'an if kinayah words are used?
A) Tafwid al-talaq using 'fawwadtu ilayka' (explicit)
B) Khul' using 'khala'tuki' without mentioning compensation (kinayah)
C) Ila' using an explicit oath 'la ajami'uki'
D) Ruju' using the explicit word 'raja'tuki'

Key Takeaway
The rule that explicit words need no intention and kinayah words need intention is applied consistently across tafwid, khul', ila', zihar, and ruju'.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
6 min
Intention Required Only for Implicit Expressions in Five Family Law Acts
States the rule that explicit expressions do not require intention, implicit do

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that explicit expressions in tafwid, khul', ila', zihar, and raj'ah do not require intention, and classify given expressions as sarih or kinayah.

Core Idea
Explicit words need no intention; implicit words do – the same distinction governs five key family law acts.

When a husband says 'raja'tuki' during the waiting period, is his intention necessary for the return to be valid? According to Hanafi law, the answer depends on whether the words are explicit or implicit. This lesson presents a key rasm rule: in five specific acts – delegation of divorce, khul', ila', zihar, and raj'ah – explicit expressions take effect regardless of intention, while implicit expressions require intention to specify the act. This distinction is practical for muftis evaluating contested claims.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Recite the rule: explicit terms in these acts need no intention; implicit terms do.
The maxim is a sub-maxim (dabt) of the major qāʿida 'al-umūr bi-maqāṣidihā'.
2
Identify the five acts
List: tafwid al-ṭalāq, khulʿ, īlāʾ, ẓihār, and rajʿah. Understand each briefly.
Rajʿah is treated like nikāḥ because it continues the marriage.
3
Distinguish sariḥ from kināyah
Sariḥ words have a single clear meaning; kināyah words have multiple possible meanings.
For example, 'anti ṭāliq' is sariḥ for divorce; 'anti imra'atī' is kināyah for rajʿah.
4
Apply to examples from the text
Go through each act's examples: in tafwid 'amruki bi-yadik' is kināyah; in khulʿ 'khalaʿtuki' without compensation is kināyah; in īlāʾ 'lā atīti maʿakī ʿalā firāsh' is kināyah; in ẓihār 'anti mithlu ummī' is kināyah.
The text explicitly gives these examples.
5
Note the ruling for rajʿah
Rajʿah follows the same logic: explicit like 'rajaʿtuki' needs no intention; implicit like 'anti imra'atī' needs intention.
This is because rajʿah is the continuation of marriage.

Source Text

اور بہر حال تفویض طلاق خلع ، ایلاء، اور ظہار سوجو ان میں سے صریح ہو اس کے لئے نیت شرط نہیں اور جو کنا یہ ہو اس کے لئے نیت شرط ہے۔ تشریح تفویض طلاق شوہر اپنی بیوی کی طلاق کے حق کو کسی دوسرے آدمی کو سونپ دے یا اپنی بیوی ہی کے پرد کرے۔ جیسے شوہر بیوی سے کہدے اختاری او امرک بیدک ۔ یاکسی دوسرے سے کہدے فوضت البک طلاق امرأتی خلع: شوہر اپنی بیوی سے مال پر ضلع کرلے مثلا شوہر بیوی سے کہے خالـعتك على كذا اور عورت قبول کرلے ایلاء یہ ہے کہ شوہر اپنی بیوی کے پاس چار ماہ یا اس سے زائد نہ جانے کی قسم کھالے جیسے لا اجـامـعک الی اربعة شهر هو عبارة عن اليمين على ترك وطئ المنكوحة اربعة اشهر او اكثر هذا في اصطلاح الفقهاء والمعنى اللغوى الايلاء بالمد الحلف - اور اظہار میں انت مثل امی او ،کامی فكما نوى لانه من الكنايات تفویض طلاق، خلع ، ایلاء، اور ظہار کے بارے میں علامہ ابن نجیم المصری فرماتے ہیں کہ جو الفاظ ان کے لئے مرتع ہیں ان میں قضاء نیت کی ضرورت نہیں ہے اور جو ان میں کنائی الفاظ ہیں ان کی صحت کے لئے قضاء نیت ضروری ہے۔ وَأَمَّا الرَّجْعَةُ فَكَالنِّكَاحِ لأَنَّهَا إِسْتِدَامَتُهُ لَكِنْ مَا كَانَ مِنْهَا صَرِيْحاً لَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهَا وكتابتها تحتاج إلَيْهَا. ترجمہ: اور رہی رجعت وہ نکاح کی طرح ہے اس لئے کہ وہ اس کی بقا کو طلب کرنا ہے لیکن اس میں سے جو رتا ہیں ان میں نیت کی حاجت نہیں اور جو ان میں کنائی ہیں ان میں نسیت لابدی ہے۔ تشريح: الرجعة لغة هي مصدر رجعه يرجعه رجعاً ورجعة وهي بمعنى الرد والاعادة اصطلاحاً عرفها الحنفية بانها استدامة الملك القائم فى العدة برد الزوجة الى زوجها واعادتها الى حالتها الأولى. رجعت در اصل بقاء نکاح کو طلب کرنے کا نام ہے صاحب کتاب فرماتے ہیں کہ جو الفاظ رجعت صرتع ہیں تو ان میں صحت رجعت کے لئے قضاء نیت ضروری نہیں ہے جیسے شوہر طلاق رجعی کے بعد حالت عدت میں یوں کہے راجعتک وردد تک اور جو الفاظ کنائی ہیں ان میں صحت رجعت کے لئے نیت ضروری ہے بدون نیست رجعت صحیح نہ ہوگی جیسے شوہر کہے انتِ امرأتی یا کہا انت عندى كما كنت.
And in all cases of tafwid, khul', ila', and zihar: whatever is explicit (sarih) does not require intention; whatever is implicit (kinayah) requires intention. Explanation: Tafwid al-talaq: husband delegates right of divorce to another or to the wife; e.g. he says 'ikhari' or 'amruki bi-yadik' or says 'fawwadtu ilayki talaqa imra'ati'. Khul': husband divorces wife for compensation; e.g. he says 'khala'tuki 'ala kadha' and wife accepts. Ila': husband swears to abstain from intercourse for four months or more. Zihar: husband says to wife 'anti 'alayya ka-zahri ummi' – likening to a mahram relative. For tafwid, kinayah examples: 'ikhari' or 'amruki bi-yadik'; for khul' kinayah: 'khala'tuki' or 'ba'atuki' without mentioning compensation; for ila' kinayah: 'la atiti ma'aki 'ala firash' etc.; for zihar kinayah: 'anti mithlu ummi' etc. 'Allamah Ibn Nujaym states: explicit terms do not require intention (qada'an), implicit terms require intention. As for raj'ah: it is like marriage because it is its continuation; what is explicit does not require intention, what is implicit does. Raj'ah linguistically means returning; technically: the continuation of existing marital bond during 'iddah by returning the wife to her husband. The author of the matn says: explicit words like 'raja'tuki' or 'radadtuki' do not need intention; implicit like 'anti imra'ati' or 'anti 'indi kama kuntu' need intention.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage establishes a key rasm rule (methodological rule) for the mufti: in acts of tafwid al-talaq, khul', ila', zihar, and raj'ah, the distinction between explicit (sarih) and implicit (kinayah) expressions determines whether intention (niyyah) is required for validity. Explicit terms are those conventionally understood without context; they are effective even without the speaker's intention. Implicit terms carry multiple possible meanings; intention is needed to specify that a dissolution or return of marriage is meant. The text enumerates examples: in tafwid, 'amruki bi-yadik' is implicit; in khul', 'khala'tuki' without mention of compensation is implicit; in ila', 'la atiti ma'aki' is implicit; in zihar, 'anti mithlu ummi' is implicit. Raj'ah follows the same logic because it is essentially the continuation of marriage. The rule is based on the qawida that effects of explicit statements are fixed by linguistic convention, while implicit statements require evidence of intent. This is fundamental for muftis evaluating such cases, especially when litigants claim alternative meanings.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A word that conveys its meaning without any ambiguity or need for context or intention.
كناية (kinayah): A word that implies the intended meaning but also can mean something else; requires intention to specify the act.
تفويض الطلاق (tafwid al-talaq): Delegation of the right to divorce by the husband to another person or to the wife.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation paid to the husband.
إيلاء (ila'): Oath of the husband to abstain from marital relations for a period of four months or more.
ظهار (zihar): Husband likening his wife to a mahram female relative, e.g., 'you are to me like my mother's back'.
رجعة (raj'ah): Returning to the wife during the waiting period after a revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following expressions is considered explicit (sarih) for īlāʾ?
A) 'lā atīti maʿakī ʿalā firāsh'
B) 'lā ajāmiʿuki ilā arbaʿati ashhur'
C) 'anti mithlu ummī'
D) 'amruki bi-yadik'

Key Takeaway
In tafwid, khul', ila', zihar, and raj'ah, explicit words are effective without intention; implicit words require intention to be valid.

Intention in Oaths by Allah: Diyanatan vs Qada'an and the Mazlum Condition
Explains that oaths by Allah do not require intention to bind; intention to narrow scope is accepted in specific cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm rule that an oath by Allah is binding without intention, and distinguish between diyanatan acceptance of intention to narrow and its qada'an restriction to the mazlum.

Core Idea
An oath by Allah binds even without intention; to narrow it, intention helps only diyanatan, and in court only if you are oppressed.

This passage lays down a critical rule for muftis: oaths by Allah are not dependent on the swearer's internal state for their validity. However, when a swearer wants to restrict a general expression in his oath, the law distinguishes between what is valid before Allah (diyanatan) and what is enforceable in court (qada'an). Moreover, the fatwa introduces an equitable condition: the swearer's intention is accepted in court only if he is oppressed, preventing abusers from evading their oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Recognise that an oath by Allah (e.g., 'Wallahi') is concluded (mun'aqid) regardless of the swearer's state: intentional, forgetful, mistaken, or coerced. The ruling is binding, and breaking it requires kaffarah.
This is a rasm rule that overrides the general maxim al-umur bi-maqasidiha for oaths by Allah.
2
Distinguish diyanatan from qada'an
Note that the intention to narrow a general expression in the oath is accepted diyanatan (before Allah) by consensus. This means the swearer is not sinful if he abides by his intended meaning, even if the literal meaning is broader.
Diyanatan applies in private conscience, not in court.
3
Identify the ikhtilaf on qada'an acceptance
There is a dispute about accepting this intention in court (qada'an). Imam al-Khassaf accepts it only if the swearer is oppressed (mazlum). The fatwa follows al-Khassaf's view.
The condition prevents an oppressor from manipulating the oath.
4
Recognise whose intention counts
A secondary dispute: should the court consider the swearer's intention or the intention of the one who administered the oath? The fatwa gives weight to the swearer's intention, again only if he is mazlum.
If the swearer is an oppressor (zalim), his intention is disregarded.
5
Synthesise the mufti's rule
When a litigant claims a restricted intention in an oath, the mufti must determine if he is mazlum. Only then can the intention be considered in a judicial context. Otherwise, the literal meaning of the oath is enforced.
This nuance is crucial for issuing sound fatwas in oath-related cases.
